Systematic Answers to Quranic Objections
The author provides a concise answer to each category of objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical scholarly responses to several common objections raised against the Quran, including linguistic and interpretive issues.

Core Idea
Classical scholars systematically refuted objections to the Quran with reasoned answers.

In this passage, the author directly addresses a series of objections that had been raised against the Quran. Rather than dismissing them, he provides concise yet thorough responses grounded in linguistic science and interpretive principles. This lesson walks through each objection and its rebuttal as presented in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation together. Notice the structure: the author directly answers each objection.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Categorize the objections
The passage addresses about six distinct objections. Try to list them: non-Arabic words, grammatical errors, reported speech not verbatim, anthropomorphic expressions, repetition, discrepancy, illusion of contradiction/falsehood/poetry.
Each answer is a separate point.
3
Examine each answer in detail
For each objection, study the author's specific rebuttal. Note how he uses principles like 'predominance' for language, 'error in the objector's own grammar', and the benefit of reflection.
These answers are classical and still used.
4
Connect to broader apologetics
Consider how these combined answers strengthen the case for the Quran's inimitability and divine origin. The passage shows that objections often stem from misunderstanding or lack of knowledge.
This is a typical kalam approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أنه لا يبعد توافق اللغتين أو جعل الكل عربياً تغليباً، وأن الخطأ إما في التخطئة على ما بين في علم النحو، وأن المجكي لا يلزم أن يكون عبارة المحكى عنه، وفي التشابه فوائد مثل مثوبة النظر، أو التوقف والتكرار ربما يكون من المحاسن والاختلاف المنفى هو تفاوت النظم بحيث يقصر عن الإعجاز ، ووهم التناقض والكذب والشعر من الجهل بعلم التفسير وبمعنى الشعر.
And the answer: It is not unlikely that the two languages coincide or that all of it is made Arabic by predominance. And the error is either in the objector's own grammatical mistake, as explained in the science of grammar. And the reported (speech) does not necessarily have to be the exact wording of the reported content. And in the anthropomorphic expressions there are benefits, such as the reward of reflection or suspending judgment. And repetition may be among the beauties. And the denied discrepancy is the disparity in composition that falls short of inimitability. And the illusion of contradiction, falsehood, and poetry comes from ignorance of the science of interpretation and the meaning of poetry.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is the author's answer to the objections listed earlier. He addresses each point: the presence of non-Arabic words is explained by possible language overlap or treating them as Arabic by predominance; grammatical errors are actually errors in the objector's own grammar; reported speech does not have to be verbatim; anthropomorphic verses have benefits like encouraging reflection; repetition can be a merit; the denied 'difference' is in overall quality, not minor variations; apparent contradictions and falsehoods stem from ignorance of tafsir and the definition of poetry.

Key Terms

التخطئة
المجكي
عبارة المحكى عنه
مثوبة النظر
التوقف
التكرار
اختلاف النظم
الإعجاز
وهم التناقض
علم التفسير
معنى الشعر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These objections against the Quran are valid and have no answers.
Correction: Classical scholars provided systematic rebuttals based on linguistic and hermeneutic principles.

Checkpoint

A) They are not actually non-Arabic
B) They are Arabic by predominance or language overlap
C) They are errors that were later corrected
D) They are deliberate challenges

Key Takeaway
Classical theological scholarship systematically addresses apparent flaws in the Quran by appealing to linguistic principles, interpretive methods, and the Quran's own literary excellence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Refuting the Claim That the Quran Contains Poetry
Refutes objection that the Quran contains poetry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key arguments used to refute the objection that the Quran contains poetry, and how this relates to the doctrine of i‘jāz.

Core Idea
The Quran's occasional metrical resemblance does not make it poetry; the claim stems from ignorance.

Some critics have argued that certain Quranic verses match poetic meters, implying the Quran is merely human poetry. This passage from Sharh al-Maqāsid responds by offering multiple rational explanations, from linguistic coincidence to the benefits of repetition, and ultimately charges the critics with ignorance of tafsīr and the true definition of poetry. Let us examine the source text to see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking, then the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note the example verse given: 18:29.
2
Identify the objection and example
The passage begins by referencing the objection: some say the Quran contains poetry, citing a specific verse. Locate that verse and understand why it might be seen as metrical.
3
List the sub-arguments
The author gives several distinct responses. Try to list at least three: linguistic coincidence, scribal error, the nature of reported speech, and the benefits of similarity.
These are not enumerated but are separate points.
4
Note the final conclusion
The passage concludes that the illusion of contradiction, falsehood, or poetry arises from ignorance of tafsir and the meaning of poetry. This is the ultimate refutation.
5
Reflect on i‘jāz
Think about how this defense preserves the Quran’s inimitability. If the Quran were poetry, it would be imitable. The author’s points show why metrical resemblance is irrelevant.
This connects the passage to the broader doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك . والشعر من كل بحر مثل قوله : (فَمَن شَاءَ فَلْيُؤْمِن وَمَن شَاءَ فَلْيَكفُرْ) والجواب : أنه لا يبعد توافق اللغتين أو جعل الكل عربياً تغليباً، وأن الخطأ إما في التخطئة على ما بين في علم النحو، وأن المجكي لا يلزم أن يكون عبارة المحكى عنه، وفي التشابه فوائد مثل مثوبة النظر، أو التوقف والتكرار ربما يكون من المحاسن والاختلاف المنفى هو تفاوت النظم بحيث يقصر عن الإعجاز ، ووهم التناقض والكذب والشعر من الجهل بعلم التفسير وبمعنى الشعر.
That. And poetry from every meter like His saying: 'So whoever wills, let him believe; and whoever wills, let him disbelieve.' And the answer: it is not far-fetched that the two languages agree, or that all is made Arabic by way of predominance, and that error is either in mispointing as explained in grammar, and that the reported speech does not necessarily have to be the exact expression of the reported speaker, and in resemblance there are benefits such as reward for reflection, or that pause and repetition may be among the merits, and the negation of difference refers to disparity of composition that falls short of inimitability, and the illusion of contradiction, falsehood, and poetry is due to ignorance of tafsir and of the meaning of poetry.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that the Quran contains poetry. The author presents the example of a verse that has a metrical structure similar to poetry. He then gives multiple answers: it is possible the languages coincide or are subsumed under Arabic; scribal errors in transmission might create resemblance; the reported speech need not be verbatim; similarity can have positive benefits like encouraging contemplation; repetition and pause have merits; and crucially, the alleged contradiction, falsehood, or poetry stems from ignorance of Quranic exegesis and the true nature of poetry. Thus, the claim is dismissed as uninformed.

Key Terms
شعر
إعجاز
تخطئة
مجكي
تشابه
تفسير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Quran contains poetry because some verses fit poetic meters.
Correction: The author shows that metrical resemblance does not imply poetry, and that such claims arise from ignorance of tafsīr and the nature of poetry.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary cause of the illusion that the Quran contains poetry?
A) The Quran's verses are intentionally poetic
B) Ignorance of tafsir and the meaning of poetry
C) Scribal errors by later transmitters
D) The Quran's use of repetition

Key Takeaway
The allegation that the Quran contains poetry is refuted by showing that metrical resemblance does not imply poetry, and that such claims arise from ignorance of Quranic exegesis and the nature of poetry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone dismisses the Quran as merely poetic, do you have the knowledge to respond with the arguments from this lesson, or would you be left speechless? What will you do this week to internalize these refutations so they become part of your defense of the Quran?
?
The lesson concludes that the illusion of poetry comes from ignorance of tafsir and the meaning of poetry. Are you actively seeking knowledge to remove that ignorance from your own heart, or are you comfortable with superficial understanding? What specific step will you take today to deepen your tafsir study?
?
The author notes that resemblance (tashābuh) in the Quran has benefits such as reward for reflection. When you recite verses that sound similar to each other, do you pause to reflect on the wisdom behind the repetition, or do you rush through? How will you cultivate a reflective habit in your daily recitation starting now?

The Quranic Challenge: Proof of Inimitability through Arab Failure
Describes the Prophet's challenge and proves the Arabs' inability to oppose

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Prophet's challenge (tahaddi) to the Arabs demonstrates the Quran's miraculous nature, and why the Arabs' inability to produce a single surah is considered certain knowledge.

Core Idea
The Prophet's challenge was a public test; the Arabs' failure is proven by their preference for war over literary competition.

This passage presents the first station of proving Quranic inimitability: the open challenge. Sanusi describes the Arabs' exceptional rhetorical skills and fierce tribal pride, yet they could not produce a single surah like the Quran. Their choice to fight instead of competing shows utter incapacity. The author then explains why this failure is known with certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once to get the flow. Notice the emphatic description of the Arabs' abilities and motives.
2
Identify the key elements of the challenge
The passage lists the Prophet's call, the Arabs' qualities, their failure, and the argument for certainty. Find each part.
3
Understand the argument for certainty
The author argues that any successful opposition would have been transmitted. Reflect on why this makes the knowledge certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقام الأول فهو أنه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم تحدى بالقرآن، ودعا إلى الإتيان بسورة مثله مصاقع البلغاء والفصحاء من العرب العرباء مع كثرتهم كثرة رمال الدهناء، وحصى البطحاء، وشهرتهم بغاية العصبية والحمية الجاهلية، وتهالكهم على المباهاة والمباراة والدفاع عن الأحساب، وركوب الشطط في هذا الباب فعجزوا حتى آثروا المقارعة على المعارضة، وبذلوا المهج والأرواح دون المدافعة فلو قدروا على المعارضة لعارضوا. ولو عارضوا لنقل إلينا لتوفر الدواعي وعدم الصارف، والعلم بجميع ذلك قطعي كسائر العاديات، لا يقدح فيه احتمال أنهم تركوا المعارضة مع القدرة عليها أو عارضوا ولم ينقل إلينا لمانع: عدم المبالاة وقلة الالتفات والاشتغال بالمهمات.
As for the first station, it is that he, may Allah's prayers and peace be upon him, challenged with the Quran, and called the eloquent and fluent ones of the pure Arabs, despite their abundance like the sands of the desert and the pebbles of the valley, and their fame for extreme partisanship and pre-Islamic zeal, and their eagerness for boasting and competition and defense of honor, and their exceeding bounds in this matter, to produce a surah like it. So they were incapable, to the point that they preferred warfare over opposition, and sacrificed lives and souls rather than competing. For if they had been able to oppose, they would have opposed. And if they had opposed, it would have been transmitted to us due to the abundance of motives and absence of deterrent. And knowledge of all this is certain like other customary matters. The possibility that they abandoned opposition despite ability, or that they opposed but it was not transmitted to us due to some impediment — such as lack of care, inattention, or preoccupation with important matters — does not detract from this.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents the first station regarding the Quran's inimitability: the Prophet's open challenge to the Arabs to produce a chapter like the Quran. The author emphasizes the Arabs' strong motivations—their rhetorical skill, tribal pride, and hostility—yet they failed. Instead of literary opposition, they chose armed conflict, which shows utter incapacity. The author then argues that if they had succeeded, it would have been widely transmitted due to overwhelming motives and no deterrent. The knowledge of this failure is certain, like other customary knowledge, and is not undermined by hypothetical scenarios of lack of care or transmission obstacles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Arabs could have opposed the Quran but simply chose not to due to disinterest.
Correction: The passage demonstrates their intense motivation and hostility; they preferred war over literary competition, proving their inability.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the Arabs' inability to oppose the Quran as certain knowledge?
A) Because the Quran says so
B) Because the motives were strong and no transmission of opposition occurred
C) Because later scholars agreed on it
D) Because the Prophet predicted it

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's challenge to the Arabs, combined with their failure to respond despite strong motives, provides certain proof of the Quran's miraculous nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were among those eloquent Arabs, hearing the Prophet's challenge, would you have honestly attempted to produce a surah like the Quran? What internal resistance might you have felt against submitting to the truth?
?
The Arabs chose warfare—sacrificing their lives—rather than face the Quran. What are you willing to sacrifice to avoid confronting the full authority of the Quran in your own life? Is there an area where you 'prefer warfare' over surrender?
?
Sanusi says the certainty of this proof is like everyday certainties (e.g., that fire burns). Are you as certain of the Quran's divine origin as you are of such simple facts? If not, what is standing between you and that level of certainty?

Two Theories of Quranic Inimitability
Presents majority view of intrinsic eloquence and al-Nazzam's sarfa theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classical theories of Quranic inimitability—the majority view of intrinsic eloquence and the sarfa theory of divine prevention—and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
The Quran's inimitability is either in its own eloquence or in God's prevention of imitation.

This passage presents the second station in the discussion of miracles: the nature of the Quran's inimitability. Two major positions are outlined—the majority view that the Quran's own supreme eloquence makes it inimitable, and the minority view (sarfa) that God actively prevents challengers from imitating it. The summary critically notes the sarfa view is not without problems.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice how the author first presents the majority view with its supporting elements, then the sarfa view with its variations.
The author uses 'wa dhahaba' to transition to the second view.
2
Identify the two theories
The passage presents two theories: (1) al-jumhur: inimitability due to supreme eloquence, (2) al-Nazzam and others: inimitability due to sarfa. Note the different aspects of the majority view (eloquence, reports of unseen, divine wisdom, ethics) and the three subtypes of sarfa (removal of power, motivation, or knowledge).
3
Understand al-Murtada's version
The passage gives special attention to al-Murtada's version of sarfa. He argued that the Arabs had the necessary knowledge of composition but Allah removed it whenever they attempted imitation. Consider why this might be problematic.
The author ends with 'fihi nazar' indicating this view is not conclusive.
4
Reflect on the critique
The author's closing remark 'fihi nazar' (there is consideration) suggests that the sarfa view has weaknesses. Think about what those might be and how the majority view might respond.
This connects to the common misunderstanding that sarfa denies the Quran's inherent excellence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثاني فالجمهور على أن إعجاز القرآن لكونه في الطبقة العليا من الفصاحة، والدرجة القصوى من البلاغة على ما يعرفه فصحاء العرب بسليقتهم، وعلماء الفرق بمهارتهم في فن البيان وإحاطتهم بأساليب الكلام. وهذا مع اشتماله على الإخبار عن المغيبات الماضية والآتية، كما سنذكره، وعلى دقائق العلوم الإلهية، وأحوال المبدأ والمعاد ومكارم الأخلاق، والإرشاد إلى فنون الحكمة العلمية والعملية والمصالح الدينية والدنيوية على ما يظهر للمتدبرين ويتجلى على المتفكرين. وذهب النظام وكثير من المعتزلة والمرتضى من الشيعة إلى أن إعجازه بالصرفة، وهي أن الله صرف همم المتحدين عن معارضته مع قدرتهم عليها، وذلك إما بسلب قدرهم، أو بسلب دواعيهم، أو بسلب العلوم التي لا بد منها في الإتيان بمثل القرآن. بمعنى أنها لم تكن حاصلة لهم، أو بمعنى أنها كانت حاصلة فأزالها الله. وهذا هو المختار عند المرتضى، وتحقيقه أنه كان عندهم العلم بنظم القرآن، والعلم بأنه كيف يؤلف كلام يساويه أو يدانيه. والمعتاد أن من كان عنده هذان العلمان يتمكن من الإتيان بالمثل، إلا أنهم كلما حاولوا ذلك، أزال الله تعالى عن قلوبهم تلك العلوم. وفيه نظر.
As for the second station, the majority holds that the inimitability of the Quran is due to it being in the highest class of eloquence and the utmost degree of rhetoric, as known to the eloquent Arabs by their natural instinct and to the scholars of various groups through their mastery of the art of rhetoric and their comprehensive knowledge of styles of speech. And this, along with its inclusion of reports of past and future unseen things, as we will mention, and the subtleties of divine sciences, the states of the beginning and the return, noble ethics, and guidance to the wisdoms of theoretical and practical philosophy, and religious and worldly interests, as appears to those who reflect and is revealed to those who ponder. And al-Nazzam and many of the Mu'tazila and al-Murtada from the Shi'a held that its inimitability is by way of sarfa, which is that Allah turned away the aspirations of the challengers from opposing it despite their ability to do so. And that is either by removing their power, or removing their motivations, or removing the knowledges necessary to produce something like the Quran. Either meaning that they did not possess those knowledges, or that they possessed them but Allah removed them. And this is the chosen view of al-Murtada. Its verification is that they had knowledge of the composition of the Quran and knowledge of how to compose speech equal to it or close to it. And the usual state is that whoever has these two knowledges can produce something similar, except that whenever they attempted that, Allah removed those knowledges from their hearts. And there is consideration in this.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents the second station: the nature of the Quran's inimitability. The majority (al-jumhur) holds that it is due to the Quran's own supreme eloquence and rhetoric, known by instinct and scholarly analysis. Additionally, the Quran contains reports of unseen, divine wisdom, guidance, and ethics. Then the author presents the view of al-Nazzam, many Mu'tazila, and al-Murtada (Shi'a) that inimitability is due to sarfa—Allah preventing the challengers from imitating by removing their ability, motivation, or knowledge. Al-Murtada's version holds that the Arabs had the necessary knowledge of style and composition, but Allah would remove that knowledge whenever they attempted imitation. The author concludes with 'fihi nazar' (there is consideration), indicating this view is not definitively accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sarfa theory denies any inherent excellence in the Quran.
Correction: The passage shows that al-Murtada's version of sarfa acknowledges that the Arabs had knowledge of the Quran's composition and could produce similar speech; thus it does not deny the Quran's eloquence, but rather explains why it was not imitated.

Checkpoint
According to the majority view, what is the basis of the Quran's inimitability?
A) Divine prevention of imitation (sarfa)
B) Its supreme eloquence and rhetoric
C) The Prophet's challenge
D) Its length and complexity

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is explained either by its own supreme eloquence and content or by divine prevention of imitation, with the latter view being subject to critique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you feel its eloquence moving your heart, or have you become so familiar that you no longer sense its inimitability? What would it take for you to be genuinely awestruck by its language today?
?
If the Quran's inimitability were only due to God's external prevention (sarfa), would your personal conviction in its divine origin be shaken? How much does your belief rely on the Quran's own excellence versus external factors?
?
Al-Murtada's theory suggests that even with knowledge of the Quran's composition, one could lose it when attempting imitation. Have you ever felt that your understanding of the Quran slips away when you try to articulate it? What does that reveal about your connection to its words?

The Majority View on the Inimitability of the Quran
Taftazani states the majority view that i'jaz lies in supreme eloquence and comprehensive content.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the majority of scholars hold that the Quran's inimitability is due to both its supreme eloquence and its profound content, including unseen news and guidance.

Core Idea
The Quran's miracle is twofold: unmatched eloquence and comprehensive truth.

Taftazani presents the mainstream Sunni position on why the Quran is miraculous. Unlike some who attribute inimitability to divine prevention (sarfah) or other factors, the majority see the miracle in the Quran itself—its language and its content. This passage establishes that the inimitability is not merely stylistic but also substantive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Note the two main parts: the claim about eloquence and the claim about content.
The passage begins with 'وأما المقام الثاني' indicating this is the second station.
2
Identify the two components of i'jaz
Find where Taftazani states the majority view. He says it is 'لكونه في الطبقة العليا من الفصاحة، والدرجة القصوى من البلاغة' (eloquence) and 'وهذا مع اشتماله على الإخبار عن المغيبات...' (content). These are the two facets.
The word 'وهذا مع' indicates the content is an additional factor.
3
Reflect on the content list
The passage lists several types of content: news of the unseen, divine sciences, states of beginning and return, noble morals, and guidance to wisdom and interests. Consider how each contributes to the Quran's miraculous nature.
This shows the miracle is not only linguistic but also informational and ethical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثاني فالجمهور على أن إعجاز القرآن لكونه في الطبقة العليا من الفصاحة، والدرجة القصوى من البلاغة على ما يعرفه فصحاء العرب بسليقتهم، وعلماء الفرق بمهارتهم في فن البيان وإحاطتهم بأساليب الكلام. وهذا مع اشتماله على الإخبار عن المغيبات الماضية والآتية، كما سنذكره، وعلى دقائق العلوم الإلهية، وأحوال المبدأ والمعاد ومكارم الأخلاق، والإرشاد إلى فنون الحكمة العلمية والعملية والمصالح الدينية والدنيوية على ما يظهر للمتدبرين ويتجلى على المتفكرين.
And as for the second station, the majority (al-jumhūr) hold that the inimitability (i'jāz) of the Qur'an is due to its being in the highest class of eloquence (al-faṣāḥa) and the utmost degree of rhetoric (al-balāgha), as the eloquent Arabs know by their natural disposition (salīqatihim) and the scholars of the sects know through their skill in the art of eloquence (al-bayān) and their encompassing knowledge of the styles of speech. And this is together with its including information about past and future unseen things, as we will mention, and subtle divine sciences, the states of the beginning and the return, noble morals, and guidance to the scientific and practical arts of wisdom, and religious and worldly interests, as appears to those who reflect and becomes manifest to those who contemplate.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the mainstream Sunni position on the i'jaz of the Qur'an. Taftazani begins by stating that the majority (al-jumhūr) hold that the Qur'an's miraculousness is due to its being at the highest level of fasaha (verbal eloquence) and the utmost degree of balagha (rhetorical effectiveness). This is recognized by the natural linguistic instinct of the Arab experts and by the systematic knowledge of scholars of various disciplines. Then he adds that this eloquence is coupled with the Qur'an's content: it reports unseen events of the past and future, contains subtle divine sciences, describes the beginning of creation and the afterlife, teaches noble ethics, and guides to both theoretical and practical wisdom, as well as religious and worldly benefits. This makes the i'jaz multifaceted.

Key Terms
إعجاز
فصاحة
بلاغة
مغيبات
الجمهور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Quran's inimitability is only due to its eloquent style.
Correction: The majority hold that it is due to both supreme eloquence and its comprehensive content, including unseen news and guidance.

Checkpoint
According to the majority view, the inimitability of the Qur'an is based on:
A) Only its eloquent style
B) Both its supreme eloquence and its comprehensive content including unseen news
C) Its being a divine diversion (sarfah)
D) The fact that no one could organize its letters

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is recognized by its supreme eloquence and its rich content, including unseen knowledge and guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the Quran's inimitability includes both its unmatched eloquence and its comprehensive content. When was the last time you paused to appreciate the Quran's language and style, not just its meaning? Do you treat the Quran as the ultimate standard in both form and content, or have you reduced it to a source of information? Commit today to read at least one verse with attention to both its beauty and its depth.
?
The passage says that the Quran contains guidance to 'religious and worldly interests' for those who reflect. Are you actively seeking that guidance in your daily decisions—your work, your relationships, your habits—or is your relationship with the Quran limited to ritual recitation? Ask yourself: what is one area of my life where I have not consulted the Quran for direction? Will you look up a relevant verse this week?
?
Taftazani states that the Quran's inimitability is recognized by 'those who reflect and contemplate.' Does your heart truly recognize the Quran as the speech of Allah, superior to all human words? Or have you become so familiar with it that you no longer feel its awe? When was the last time you were genuinely moved by its power? Pray to Allah to restore the freshness of the Quran in your heart, and start a habit of reflecting on a single verse each day.

The Theory of Al-Sarfah: Divine Diversion as Miracle
Taftazani presents and critiques the Mu'tazilite and Shi'ite theory of sarfah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite and Shi'ite theory of al-sarfah and Taftazani's critical response.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's inimitability is not in its text but in God's act of diverting challengers.

This passage presents an alternative explanation for the Qur'an's inimitability. Instead of focusing on the text itself, al-Nazzam and others argued that the miracle lies in God's intervention to prevent the Arabs from producing a similar work. Taftazani explains the theory in detail and then signals his disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
Pay special attention to the phrase 'Wa fihi nazar' at the end.
2
Identify the three modes of diversion
The passage lists three ways God could divert the challengers: by removing power, motives, or knowledge. Distinguish between the two variants regarding knowledge.
Al-Murtada's chosen version involves acquired knowledge being removed.
3
Observe Taftazani's critique
After presenting the theory, Taftazani simply says 'Wa fihi nazar' (there is criticism of this). Reflect on why this theory might be problematic from the perspective of the standard view of i'jaz.
Consider how this theory might undermine the idea of the Qur'an's textual supremacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب النظام وكثير من المعتزلة والمرتضى من الشيعة إلى أن إعجازه بالصرفة وهي أن الله صرف همم المتحدين عن معارضته مع قدرتهم عليها، وذلك إما بسلب قدرهم، أو بسلب دواعيهم، أو بسلب العلوم التي لا بد منها في الإتيان بمثل القرآن. بمعنى أنها لم تكن حاصلة لهم، أو بمعنى أنها كانت حاصلة فأزالها الله. وهذا هو المختار عند المرتضى، وتحقيقه أنه كان عندهم العلم بنظم القرآن، والعلم بأنه كيف يؤلف كلام يساويه أو يدانيه. والمعتاد أن من كان عنده هذان العلمان يتمكن من الإتيان بالمثل، إلا أنهم كلما حاولوا ذلك، أزال الله تعالى عن قلوبهم تلك العلوم. وفيه نظر.
And al-Nazzām (al-Naẓẓām) and many of the Mu'tazila and al-Murtaḍā from the Shī'a held that its inimitability is by the diversion (al-ṣarfa), which is that God diverted the ambitions of the challengers from opposing it while they had the power to do so. That is either by stripping them of their power, or stripping them of their motives, or stripping them of the knowledge necessary to produce something like the Qur'an. Meaning that it was not acquired by them, or that it was acquired and God removed it. This is the chosen view of al-Murtaḍā. Its verification is that they had knowledge of the composition of the Qur'an and knowledge of how to compose speech that equals it or approximates it. And it is customary that whoever possesses these two types of knowledge is capable of producing a similar work, except that whenever they attempted that, God Almighty removed those sciences from their hearts. But there is criticism of this.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents a major alternative theory of i'jaz. Taftazani reports that al-Nazzam (a prominent Mu'tazilite) and many Mu'tazila, as well as al-Murtada (a Shi'ite scholar), held that the Qur'an's miracle is due to al-sarfah, meaning that God diverted the challengers from attempting to produce a similar text, despite their ability. The diversion could be by removing their power, their motivation, or the necessary knowledge. There are two variants: either the knowledge of how to compose such speech was never granted to them, or it was granted and then removed. al-Murtada's version is that the Arabs had knowledge of the Qur'an's composition and of how to produce comparable speech, but whenever they tried, God removed that knowledge from their hearts. Taftazani ends with the remark 'there is criticism of this,' signaling rejection.

Key Terms
الصرفة
النظام
المرتضى
المعتزلة
الشيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The theory of al-sarfah denies the miraculous nature of the Qur'anic text itself.
Correction: It still asserts divine intervention as the cause of inimitability, though it shifts the miracle from the text to God's action.

Checkpoint
According to the theory of al-sarfah, what makes the Qur'an inimitable?
A) The Qur'an's own unique rhetorical features
B) God's act of diverting the challengers from producing a similar text
C) The Arabs' lack of intelligence
D) The Qur'an's musical rhythm

Key Takeaway
The theory of al-sarfah posits that the miracle of the Qur'an lies in God's diversion of challengers, but Taftazani indicates this view is untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered the possibility that the Qur'an's miracle might depend on God actively preventing imitation rather than the inherent power of its words? How does this challenge or deepen your conviction in the Qur'an's unique nature?
?
If the miracle were merely a matter of divine diversion, would your trust in the Qur'an as a direct and permanent proof of prophethood be shaken? Examine your heart: do you rely on the text itself or on external signs?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you sense its inimitability? Or do you take it for granted, assuming God simply protects it from challenge? What would it mean for your faith if the miracle were only in God's intervention and not in the text?

First Argument for Al-Sarfah and Its Refutation
Taftazani refutes the claim that Arabs could produce short Qur'anic phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument used by proponents of al-sarfah and how Taftazani refutes it by distinguishing between judgments on wholes and parts.

Core Idea
The whole is not merely the sum of its parts.

Proponents of al-sarfah claimed that since eloquent Arabs could produce short phrases similar to those in the Qur'an, they could have produced the entire Qur'an. Taftazani counters this by pointing out a logical fallacy: the ruling on a complete composition differs from the ruling on its isolated components. In this lesson, you will read the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is stated. Notice how the argument moves from short phrases to the whole Qur'an.
2
Identify the refutation's key principle
Taftazani states that the judgment of a whole may differ from the judgment of its parts. Make sure you understand why this refutes the objection.
3
See the analogy with poetry
Taftazani strengthens his refutation by an analogy: if the objection were valid, any Arab could produce poetry like Imru' al-Qays. Recognize why this comparison is effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجوا أولاً بأنا نقطع بأن فصحاء العرب كانوا قادرين على التكلم بمثل مفردات الصورة ومركباتها القصيرة مثل: «الحمد لله» ومثل: «رب العالمين» وهكذا، إلى الآخر، فيكونون قادرين على الإتيان بمثل الصورة. وثانياً بأن الصحابة عند جمع القرآن كانوا يتوقفون في بعض السور والآيات إلى شهادة الثقات، وابن مسعود رضي الله تعالى عنه قد بقي متردداً في الفاتحة والمعوذتين، ولو كان نظم القرآن معجزاً بفصاحته لكان كافياً في الشهادة. والجواب عن الأول بأن حكم الجملة قد يخالف حكم الأجزاء. وهذه بعينها شبهة من نفى قطيعة الإجماع والخبر المتواتر، ولو صح ما ذكر لكان كل من آحاد العرب قادراً على الإتيان بمثل قصائد فصحائهم، كامرىء القيس وأضرابه، واللازم قطعي البطلان. وعن الثاني بعد صحة الرواية وكون الجمع بعد النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم لا في زمانه، وكون كل سورة مستقلة بالإعجاز أن ذلك كان للاحتياط، والاحتراز عن أدنى تغيير لا يخل بالإعجاز، وأن إعجاز كل سورة ليس مما يظهر لكل أحد بحيث لا يبقى له تردد أصلا.
And they argued first that we are certain that the eloquent Arabs were able to speak like the individual components of the form and its short compounds, such as 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' and 'rabbi l-ʿālamīn', and so on, to the extent that they were able to produce a similar form. Secondly, that the Companions during the compilation of the Qur'an used to hesitate about some chapters and verses until the testimony of reliable witnesses, and Ibn Masʿūd (may God be pleased with him) remained undecided about al-Fātiḥa and al-Muʿawwidhatayn. If the composition of the Qur'an were miraculous by its eloquence, it would have sufficed for testimony. The answer to the first is that the judgment of a whole may differ from the judgment of its parts. And this is precisely the same doubt as that of one who denies the certainty of consensus and the mutawātir report. If what was mentioned were correct, then every individual Arab would be able to produce poems like those of their eloquent poets, such as Imru' al-Qays and his peers, and the consequent is certainly false. As for the second, after the validity of the narration and that the compilation took place after the Prophet (may God bless him and grant him peace), not during his time, and that each chapter is independently miraculous, that was for caution and to guard against the slightest change that does not affect the i'jaz, and that the i'jaz of each chapter is not something that appears to everyone such that he would have no doubt at all.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents two objections raised by proponents of al-sarfah against the intrinsic i'jaz view, followed by their refutations. The first objection: since Arabs could produce short phrases like 'al-hamdu lillah' and 'rabbi l-‘alamin', they could produce the whole Qur'an. Taftazani answers that the ruling on the whole differs from the ruling on its parts; this is similar to the sophistry of those who deny the certainty of consensus and khabar mutawatir. If it were valid, then any Arab could produce poetry like Imru' al-Qays. The consequent is false. The second objection: during the compilation, Companions hesitated about some surahs, and Ibn Mas'ud doubted al-Fatiha and al-Mu'awwidhatayn. If the text were inherently miraculous, testimony would be unnecessary. The answer: after confirming the narration's authenticity and noting that the compilation was after the Prophet's death, and that each surah is independently miraculous, the hesitation was for extreme caution to prevent even minor changes that do not affect i'jaz. Also, the i'jaz of each surah is not evident to everyone without any doubt.

Key Terms
احتجاج
الجواب
شبهة
قطيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since Arabs could produce short Qur'anic phrases, they could produce the entire Qur'an.
Correction: The judgment on a whole composition is not the same as on its parts; a short phrase does not imply ability to produce a coherent book.

Checkpoint
What principle does Taftazani use to refute the first objection?
A) The whole differs from its parts
B) Arabs were not eloquent
C) Short phrases are not like Qur'an
D) The Qur'an has no short phrases

Key Takeaway
Producing a short phrase does not imply the ability to produce a complete miraculous composition.

Second Argument for Al-Sarfah and Its Refutation
Taftazani refutes the claim that Companions' hesitation shows lack of i'jaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the intrinsic i'jaz of the Qur'an based on the Companions' hesitation during compilation and Taftazani's multi-part refutation.

Core Idea
Companions' caution does not undermine the Qur'an's inherent miracle.

The second objection points to historical incidents where Companions hesitated about certain surahs and verses, suggesting that if the Qur'an were intrinsically miraculous, no such hesitation would occur. Taftazani responds with several points: the narration's authenticity, the timing of compilation, the independence of each surah's miracle, and the reason for caution. This lesson examines that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the passage containing the second objection and Taftazani's answer. Note the structure of the refutation.
2
Identify the components of the refutation
Taftazani gives multiple reasons. List the distinct points he makes: authenticity of narration, compilation after Prophet, independence of surahs, caution, and limited visibility of i'jaz.
This step helps in understanding the layered response.
3
Understand why hesitation was due to caution
Recognize that the Companions were not doubting the Qur'an's miraculous nature but were taking extreme care to preserve every letter.
4
Recognize that i'jaz may not be apparent to all
Taftazani notes that the miraculous aspect of each surah is not self-evident to everyone without any doubt. This explains why testimony was sought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Four Unsuccessful Theories of I'jaz
Taftazani lists and refutes four partial theories of i'jaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why four common theories of the Qur'an's inimitability (i'jaz) are insufficient, and the reasoning behind Taftazani's refutation.

Core Idea
No single literary or intellectual feature alone explains the Qur'an's inimitability.

Taftazani presents four alternative explanations for the Qur'an's miraculous nature that some scholars have proposed. At first glance, each seems plausible. However, he shows that each feature can be found outside the Qur'an, thereby demonstrating that none can serve as the sole basis for i'jaz. Let's examine these theories and the refutation he offers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four theories
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the four different views that begin with 'قيل'.
These views are attributed to some scholars, but Taftazani will refute them.
2
Notice the common refutation
After listing the four theories, Taftazani provides a single refutation that applies to all: each feature is not unique to the Qur'an. Identify the examples he gives: Musaylima, eloquent speech, wise speech, and books that predict the unseen.
The refutation is structured as 'ورد بأن' and then the examples.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this refutation is effective. If a proposed basis for i'jaz is found elsewhere, it cannot be the defining miracle. This suggests the true i'jaz must be something more comprehensive or a combination of factors.
Taftazani does not state his own positive theory here; he only clears the ground.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : إعجازه بنظمه الغريب المخالف لما عليه كلام العرب في الخطب والرسائل والأشعار. وقيل : بسلامته عن الاختلاف والتناقض. وقيل : باشتماله على دقائق العلوم وحقائق الحكم والمصالح. وقيل : بإخباره عن المغيبات. ورد بأن حماقات مسيلمة ومن يجري مجراه أيضاً على ذلك النظم، وبأنه كثيراً ما يسلم كلام البلغاء عن الاختلاف والتناقض، ويشتمل كلام الحكماء على العلوم والحقائق، والإخبار عن المغيبات التي لا توجد إلا في قليل من الكتاب.
And it was said: its inimitability is by its strange composition that differs from the style of Arab speech in sermons, letters, and poems. And it was said: by its being free from contradiction and discrepancy. And it was said: by its inclusion of subtle sciences and truths of wisdom and benefits. And it was said: by its reporting of unseen things. But it is refuted that the absurdities of Musaylima and those like him also follow that composition, and that frequently the speech of eloquent people is free from contradiction and discrepancy, and the speech of wise people includes sciences and truths, and reporting of unseen things is found only in a few books.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage enumerates four alternative views on the basis of i'jaz and then offers a refutation common to all. Each view reduces i'jaz to a single feature: (1) the unique composition (nazm) unlike ordinary Arab speech; (2) freedom from internal contradiction; (3) inclusion of subtle sciences and truths; (4) prediction of unseen events. The refutation is that these features are not exclusive to the Qur'an. For example, Musaylima's nonsense also has a certain composition; eloquent poets and orators often produce consistent speech; wise philosophers include deep knowledge; and predictions of the unseen appear in a few books. Therefore, none of these alone suffice to explain i'jaz.

Key Terms
نظم
سلامة عن الاختلاف
دقائق العلوم
إخبار عن المغيبات
مسيلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'an is inimitable because of its unique composition (or any single feature).
Correction: Taftazani shows that each feature is not unique to the Qur'an, so none alone accounts for i'jaz.

Checkpoint
What common refutation does Taftazani give against the four theories?
A) They are all false because the Qur'an has none of these features
B) Each feature is also present in non-miraculous speech
C) The theories contradict each other
D) The Qur'an's i'jaz is not based on any of these

Key Takeaway
Any theory of i'jaz that relies on a single feature fails because that feature is not exclusive to the Qur'an.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on why you believe the Qur'an is from Allah, do you tend to focus on one aspect—like its prophecies or its scientific accuracy—while neglecting its overall inimitability? Are you relying on a 'partial theory' of i'jaz in your own heart?
?
Taftazani shows that even the absurd words of Musaylima can have a certain 'composition.' Have you ever been impressed by eloquent but false speech, and how does that make you value the true, unique miracle of the Qur'an more?
?
If the Qur'an's inimitability cannot be pinned down to any single feature, what does that require of your relationship with it? Are you content to know it is miraculous, or are you actively seeking to experience its uniqueness in your life in a way that transforms your faith?

First Proof: Arabs Admired the Quran's Eloquence
First proof: Arabs admired Quran's eloquence, not its inimitable difficulty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against ṣarfah: that the Arabs' admiration was for the Quran's inherent eloquence, not for their inability to imitate it.

Core Idea
The Quran's eloquence itself amazed the Arabs, not divine prevention of imitation.

The first argument against ṣarfah focuses on historical evidence: the eloquent Arabs were struck by the beauty and majesty of the Quran's style. Their physical reaction—swaying their heads—shows their awe was due to the Quran's intrinsic qualities, not because they were prevented from producing a rival.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the Arabs' reaction to the Quran's style. Notice the specific qualities mentioned: beauty, eloquence, fluency, and majestic style.
The verse example 'O earth, swallow your water' illustrates a moment of awe.
2
Identify the cause of amazement
The proof states the Arabs were amazed by the Quran's composition and eloquence, not by any external prevention. Contrast this with ṣarfah's claim that the miracle is the prevention itself.
3
Understand the logical implication
If ṣarfah were true, the Arabs would not have been amazed by eloquence but by their inability to imitate. The historical evidence contradicts this.
This shows the miracle lies in the Quran's content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد استدل على بطلان الصرفة بوجوه:
الأول: أن فصحاء العرب إنما كانوا يتعجبون من حسن نظمه وبلاغته وسلاسته في جزالته، ويرقصون رؤوسهم عند سماع قوله تعالى: وَقِيلَ يَا أَرْضُ ابْلَعِي مَاءَكِ .. الآية. لذلك، لا لعدم تأتي المعارضة مع سهولتها في نفسها.
الثاني: أنه لو قصد الإعجاز بالصرفة لكان الأنسب ترك الاعتناء ببلاغته وعلو طبقته، لأن كلما كان أنزل في البلاغة وأدخل في الركاكة، كان عدم تيسر المعارضة أبلغ في خرق العادة.
الثالث: قوله تعالى: قُلْ لَئِنِ اجْتَمَعَتِ الإِنْسُ وَالْجِنُّ عَلَى أَنْ يَأْتُوا بِمِثْلِ هَذَا الْقُرْآنِ لَا يَأْتُونَ بِمِثْلِهِ وَلَوْ كَانَ بَعْضُهُمْ لِبَعْضٍ ظَهِيرًا. فإن ذكر الاجتماع والاستظهار بالغير في مقام التحدي إنما يحسن فيما لا يكون مقدوراً للبعض، ويتوهم كونه مقدوراً للكل، فيقصد نفي ذلك.
And he argued for the invalidity of ṣarfah (the theory that God prevented the Arabs from matching the Qur'an) by several proofs:
The first: That the eloquent Arabs were only amazed at the beauty of its composition, its eloquence, and its fluency in its majestic style, and they would sway their heads when hearing His statement: 'And it was said: O earth, swallow your water...' – for that, not because of the impossibility of producing a rival while it was easy in itself.
The second: That if miraculousness were intended by ṣarfah, then it would be more appropriate to neglect care for its eloquence and high level, because the lower it is in eloquence and the more it includes weakness, the more the inability to produce a rival would be a greater breaking of custom.
The third: His statement: 'Say: If mankind and jinn gathered together to produce the like of this Qur'an, they could not produce its like, even if they were helpers one to another.' For mentioning gathering and seeking help from others in the context of challenge is only appropriate in that which is not possible for some, and it is imagined to be possible for all, so He intends to negate that.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs against the theory of ṣarfah (the claim that the Quran's miraculousness consisted in God preventing the Arabs from matching it, rather than the Quran's own eloquence). The first proof: The eloquent Arabs were amazed by the Quran's beauty, eloquence, and fluency in its majestic style (e.g., the verse about the earth swallowing water). Their amazement was due to the Quran's inherent qualities, not because they could not produce a rival while it was easy in itself. The second proof: If the miracle were simply the prevention (ṣarfah), then it would be more impressive if the Quran were weak in eloquence, because the inability to match a weak text would be a greater wonder. But God made the Quran supremely eloquent, showing the miracle is in its content. The third proof: The verse from Surah al-Isra' challenges all humans and jinn to produce something like the Quran, even with mutual help. If ṣarfah were the cause, the challenge would be meaningless; the challenge implies that the Quran's eloquence itself is unattainable, and the mention of collective effort aims to negate any assumption that it could be matched by cooperation.

Key Terms
صرفة
إعجاز
فصحاء العرب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Quran's miraculousness is solely the divine prevention of imitation (ṣarfah).
Correction: This proof shows that the Arabs were amazed by the Quran's eloquence, contradicting the idea that ṣarfah was the cause of their admiration.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what caused the Arabs to admire the Quran?
A) The difficulty of imitating it
B) Its beautiful composition and eloquence
C) The lack of any rival
D) The divine prevention of imitation

Key Takeaway
The first proof demonstrates that the Quran's own beautiful composition is what made it miraculous, not an external prevention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you find yourself genuinely moved by its beauty and eloquence, or has it become routine? What would it mean for your faith if you sought out that amazement—swaying your head like the Arabs—every time you recite or hear a verse?
?
The proof shows that the Arabs' amazement was because of the Quran's own qualities, not because they were forced. Are you drawn to Allah's speech because of its intrinsic power, or do you sometimes rely on external reminders or habits? How can you cultivate a deeper appreciation for the Quran's eloquence in your daily life?
?
If the Quran's eloquence alone is enough to inspire awe, what excuse is there for negligence in reciting or reflecting upon it? How will you change your relationship with the Book so that it becomes the source of your wonder, not just your obligation?

Second Proof: Ṣarfah Would Favor Weak Eloquence
Second proof: ṣarfah would require low eloquence, but Quran is elevated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against ṣarfah: that if miraculousness were due to prevention, it would be more impressive if the Quran were less eloquent, but it is supremely eloquent.

Core Idea
If ṣarfah were true, a less eloquent Quran would be more miraculous, yet the Quran is supremely eloquent.

The second proof uses a logical reductio ad absurdum. If the miracle were simply God's prevention of imitation, then it would be more striking if the Quran were weak and uneloquent—because the inability to match a weak text would be a greater wonder. But God made the Quran supremely eloquent, showing the miracle is in its content.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It presents a conditional argument: if ṣarfah were the intended miracle, then a less eloquent Quran would be more fitting. Reflect on why that would be.
The proof challenges the very concept of ṣarfah by exposing its implications.
2
Analyze the logic
The argument: If the miracle is the prevention (not the text itself), then making the text less eloquent would make the prevention more impressive—because it would be harder to explain why no one could match a weak text. But the Quran is highly eloquent, so the miracle must be the text's own inimitability.
This is a 'what if' scenario that shows ṣarfah leads to absurdity.
3
Contrast with reality
Reality: The Quran is at the highest level of eloquence. This disproves the ṣarfah hypothesis because the actual eloquence is what makes it miraculous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد استدل على بطلان الصرفة بوجوه:
الأول: أن فصحاء العرب إنما كانوا يتعجبون من حسن نظمه وبلاغته وسلاسته في جزالته، ويرقصون رؤوسهم عند سماع قوله تعالى: وَقِيلَ يَا أَرْضُ ابْلَعِي مَاءَكِ .. الآية. لذلك، لا لعدم تأتي المعارضة مع سهولتها في نفسها.
الثاني: أنه لو قصد الإعجاز بالصرفة لكان الأنسب ترك الاعتناء ببلاغته وعلو طبقته، لأن كلما كان أنزل في البلاغة وأدخل في الركاكة، كان عدم تيسر المعارضة أبلغ في خرق العادة.
الثالث: قوله تعالى: قُلْ لَئِنِ اجْتَمَعَتِ الإِنْسُ وَالْجِنُّ عَلَى أَنْ يَأْتُوا بِمِثْلِ هَذَا الْقُرْآنِ لَا يَأْتُونَ بِمِثْلِهِ وَلَوْ كَانَ بَعْضُهُمْ لِبَعْضٍ ظَهِيرًا. فإن ذكر الاجتماع والاستظهار بالغير في مقام التحدي إنما يحسن فيما لا يكون مقدوراً للبعض، ويتوهم كونه مقدوراً للكل، فيقصد نفي ذلك.
And he argued for the invalidity of ṣarfah (the theory that God prevented the Arabs from matching the Qur'an) by several proofs:
The first: That the eloquent Arabs were only amazed at the beauty of its composition, its eloquence, and its fluency in its majestic style, and they would sway their heads when hearing His statement: 'And it was said: O earth, swallow your water...' – for that, not because of the impossibility of producing a rival while it was easy in itself.
The second: That if miraculousness were intended by ṣarfah, then it would be more appropriate to neglect care for its eloquence and high level, because the lower it is in eloquence and the more it includes weakness, the more the inability to produce a rival would be a greater breaking of custom.
The third: His statement: 'Say: If mankind and jinn gathered together to produce the like of this Qur'an, they could not produce its like, even if they were helpers one to another.' For mentioning gathering and seeking help from others in the context of challenge is only appropriate in that which is not possible for some, and it is imagined to be possible for all, so He intends to negate that.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs against the theory of ṣarfah (the claim that the Quran's miraculousness consisted in God preventing the Arabs from matching it, rather than the Quran's own eloquence). The first proof: The eloquent Arabs were amazed by the Quran's beauty, eloquence, and fluency in its majestic style (e.g., the verse about the earth swallowing water). Their amazement was due to the Quran's inherent qualities, not because they could not produce a rival while it was easy in itself. The second proof: If the miracle were simply the prevention (ṣarfah), then it would be more impressive if the Quran were weak in eloquence, because the inability to match a weak text would be a greater wonder. But God made the Quran supremely eloquent, showing the miracle is in its content. The third proof: The verse from Surah al-Isra' challenges all humans and jinn to produce something like the Quran, even with mutual help. If ṣarfah were the cause, the challenge would be meaningless; the challenge implies that the Quran's eloquence itself is unattainable, and the mention of collective effort aims to negate any assumption that it could be matched by cooperation.

Key Terms
صرفة
إعجاز
بلاغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that ṣarfah is the only explanation for the Quran's inimitability.
Correction: This proof shows that ṣarfah would actually require a less eloquent Quran, which contradicts reality.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what would be more appropriate if ṣarfah were the cause of i'jāz?
A) The Quran should be highly eloquent to show God's power
B) The Quran should be less eloquent to make prevention more striking
C) The Arabs should have been able to imitate it easily
D) The Quran should match the style of other scriptures

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that ṣarfah is incompatible with the Quran's supreme eloquence; the miracle is the text itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you sense its unique eloquence, or do you take it for granted? This proof argues that its beauty is essential to its miracle. How does that challenge you to listen more deeply?
?
If you have ever doubted that the Quran is from God, does this logical argument remove that doubt? What would it take for you to say, 'The Quran's own words are enough evidence for me'?

Third Proof: The Challenge Verse Assumes Inherent Inimitability
Third proof: challenge verse shows Quran's inherent inimitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof against ṣarfah: that the challenge verse implies the Quran is inherently inimitable, not that God simply prevents imitation.

Core Idea
The challenge to all humans and jinn makes sense only if the Quran itself is beyond their ability.

The third proof draws on the famous challenge verse where Allah challenges all of humanity and jinn to produce something like the Quran, even with mutual help. The proof argues that such a challenge is meaningful only if the Quran's inimitability is inherent—not due to external prevention. For if ṣarfah were true, the challenge would be meaningless because God could simply prevent them.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof passage
Read the Arabic passage that quotes the challenge verse from Surah al-Isra'. Note the phrase 'even if they were helpers one to another'.
2
Understand the implication of the challenge
The proof explains that mentioning collective effort in a challenge is only fitting when the task is theoretically possible for a group but not for an individual. This suggests the Quran is inherently beyond anyone's capacity.
If ṣarfah were true, the challenge would be trivial because God could simply prevent anyone from succeeding.
3
Connect to the refutation of ṣarfah
The verse intends to negate any thought that the Quran could be matched by cooperation. This shows the inimitability is in the Quran's nature, not in a temporary divine act.

The Wisdom Behind Varying Quranic Eloquence
Objection and answer on varying Quranic eloquence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the varying eloquence of Quranic verses actually strengthens the argument for its miraculous nature, and how the analogy of the craftsman explains this.

Core Idea
Varying eloquence is not a weakness; it's a deliberate strategy to make the challenge more compelling.

In this passage, an objection is raised: if the Quran's miracle is its eloquence, why aren't all verses equally eloquent? The answer reveals a subtle wisdom: by presenting some verses at a lower level of eloquence, God makes the challenge seem more attainable to the Arabs, so their failure to match even those verses becomes a stronger proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection (فإن قيل) in Arabic and translation. Understand why someone might think all verses should be equally eloquent.
2
Identify key terms
Note the terms إعجاز (miraculousness), بلاغة (eloquence), and خرق العادة (breaking custom). These are central to the argument.
3
Analyze the answer and analogy
Study the answer (قلنا) and the craftsman analogy. Explain how the analogy refutes the objection.
The analogy shows that showing less than full ability can make the challenge more striking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لو كان القصد إلى الإعجاز بالبلاغة لكان ينبغي أن يؤتى بالكل في أعلى الطبقات لكونه أبلغ في خرق العادة، والمذهب أن الله تعالى قادر على أن يأتي بما هو أفصح مما أتى به وأبلغ، وأن بعض الآيات في باب البلاغة أعلى وأرفع كقوله تعالى: وَقِيلَ يَا أَرْضُ ابْلَعِي مَاءَكِ الآية بالنسبة إلى سورة الكافرين مثلاً وقل يا أيها الكافرون.
قلنا: هذا أونى بالغرض، وأوضح في المقصود بمنزلة صانع يبرز من مصنوعاته ما ليس غاية مقدوره ونهاية ميسوره، ثم يدعو جماهير الحذاق في الصناعة إلى أن يأتوا بما يوازي أو يداني دون ما ألقاه، وأهون ما أبداه.
If it is said: If the intention were miraculousness by eloquence, then it would be necessary to bring the whole in the highest levels because that would be more effective in breaking custom, and the doctrine is that God is able to bring something more eloquent and more sublime than what He brought, and that some verses in the category of eloquence are higher and loftier, like His statement: 'And it was said: O earth, swallow your water...' in comparison to Surah al-Kafirun, for example, and 'Say: O disbelievers'.
We said: This is more fit for the purpose, and clearer in the intended, like a craftsman who brings forth from his works something that is not the utmost of his ability nor the limit of his capacity, then he invites the masses of the skilled in the craft to produce something that matches or approaches it, excluding what he has thrown down and the least of what he has shown.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the varying levels of eloquence in the Quran. The objection: If the Quran's miraculousness lies in its eloquence, then God should have made all verses at the highest level of eloquence to maximize the miracle. Since He can do so, why are some verses considered less eloquent (e.g., the verse from Hud is higher than Surah al-Kafirun)? The answer uses an analogy: It is like a master craftsman who presents a work that is not his utmost skill, then challenges the experts to produce its equal or something close, while excluding his own lesser works. By not showing his highest ability, he makes the challenge more attainable in the eyes of the challengers, thus making their failure more evident. Similarly, God intentionally varied the eloquence to make the challenge seem possible to the Arabs, so their inability to produce even the less eloquent verses proves the miracle more convincingly.

Key Terms
إعجاز
بلاغة
خرق العادة
حذاق
صانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that varying eloquence means some Quranic verses are not miraculous or that God was unable to make them equally eloquent.
Correction: The variation is intentional; it strengthens the challenge by making it more credible to the Arabs that they could produce something like it, thereby making their inability more remarkable.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why are some Quranic verses not at the highest level of eloquence?
A) God is not capable of making all verses equally eloquent
B) To make the challenge more attainable for opponents
C) Those verses are actually more eloquent
D) The Arabic language has limitations

Key Takeaway
The varying eloquence levels in the Quran are a deliberate part of its miraculous challenge, not a flaw.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever looked at the Quran and wondered why some passages seem more eloquent than others? How does this lesson challenge that doubt and call you to trust God's wisdom in every verse?
?
The craftsman held back his best to make the challenge more powerful. In your own life, are there areas where you demand that God act in the most obvious or impressive way? What would it mean for you to accept that His ways, even when subtle, are perfectly designed for your growth?
?
The objection in this lesson came from people who assumed they knew what a miracle should look like. Where in your heart do you still set conditions for God's signs? Will you repent of that presumption and submit to His design?

The Third Proof: The Enemies' Failure to Criticize the Quran
The author introduces the third rank of evidence: the inability of Arabs and later enemies to find flaws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical evidence that the Arabs, despite their expertise in rhetoric and enmity, could not find any fault in the Quran, and how later heretics invented weak criticisms.

Core Idea
The Quran's eloquence silenced even its worst enemies.

We now turn to the third rank of evidence for the miraculous nature of the Quran. Even the most skilled Arab rhetoricians, who hated Islam, could not produce a single valid criticism. They could only call it magic in their bewilderment. Later critics fared no better, inventing objections that are merely laughable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the historical context.
2
Identify the key witnesses
Notice who the witnesses are: the Arab nobles with expertise in rhetoric and extreme enmity. Their inability to find fault is significant.
3
Understand their response
They attributed it to magic and chose war over verbal challenge. Why? Because they were confounded.
This shows their admission of its unmatched eloquence.
4
Contrast with later critics
See that later heretics invented weak criticisms that are merely mockery. This contrasts with the initial silence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثالث : فأشراف العرب مع كمال حذاقتهم في أسرار الكلام ، وفرط عداوتهم للإسلام لم يجدوا فيه للطعن مجالاً، ولم يوردوا في القدح مقالاً، ونسبوه إلى السحر على ما هو دأب المحجوج المبهوت تعجباً من نظمه وبلاغته واعترفوا بأنه ليس من جنس خطب الخطباء أو شعر الشعراء، وأن له حلاوة، وعليه طلاوة وأن أسافله مغدقة، وأعاليه مثمرة، فآثروا المقارعة على المعارضة، والمقاتلة على المقاولة، وأبى الله إلا أن يتم نوره على كره المشركين ورغم المعاندين، وحين انتهى الأمر إلى من بعدهم من أعداء الدين، وفرق الملحدين اخترعوا مطاعين ليست إلا هزءة للساخرين، وضحكة للناظرين
As for the third rank: The nobles of the Arabs, despite their complete expertise in the secrets of speech and their extreme enmity to Islam, found no room for criticism in it, nor did they produce any word of disparagement. They attributed it to magic, as is the custom of the confounded and bewildered, out of wonder at its composition and eloquence. They acknowledged that it is not of the kind of orators' speeches or poets' poems, and that it possesses sweetness and has splendor, and that its lower parts are abundant and its upper parts fruitful. So they preferred confrontation over contestation, and fighting over argumentation. But God refused except to complete His light, despite the dislike of the polytheists and the spite of the obstinate. When the matter eventually reached those after them from the enemies of religion and the sects of heretics, they invented criticisms that are nothing but mockery for the scoffers and a laughingstock for the beholders.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage introduces the third rank of evidence for the miraculous nature of the Quran (i'jaz). The author explains that even the most eloquent Arabs, despite their expertise in rhetoric and their hatred of Islam, could find no fault in the Quran. They were forced to admit its beauty and eloquence, calling it magic because they were baffled. Instead of attempting a verbal challenge (mu'arada), they resorted to war. Later enemies of Islam, including heretical sects, invented weak criticisms that are merely laughable. This sets the stage for the specific objections that follow.

Key Terms
المقام الثالث
إعجاز
مطاعن
ملحدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the Arabs of the Quraysh attribute the Quran to magic?
A) Because they believed it was sorcery.
B) Because they were confounded by its eloquence and could not find any other fault.
C) Because the Prophet practiced magic.
D) Because it contained unknown words.

Key Takeaway
The Arabs' inability to critique the Quran, despite their expertise and enmity, is a powerful testimony to its divine origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Arab polytheists recognized the Quran's beauty and truth yet chose war over submission. Is there a truth in your life that you acknowledge but resist acting on? What is holding you back from surrendering to it today?
?
When you hear objections to the Quran from skeptics, do you brush them off or honestly investigate? If they prove trivial or laughable, does that deepen your conviction? If not, what would it take for you to respond like the early Arabs—with silent acknowledgment?
?
You are a human being with a soul—do you actually believe the Quran is the unmatched word of God? How does that belief affect the way you read it? If it doesn't move you to awe and obedience, ask yourself: am I being honest with myself, or am I just treating it like any other book?

Non-Arabic Words in the Quran? Refutation Explained
Objection and three refutations regarding non-Arabic words in the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three refutations scholars provide for the presence of non-Arabic words in the Quran.

Core Idea
The Quran is Arabic in composition and predominance, not necessarily every word.

A common objection against the Quran's claim to be an Arabic revelation is that it contains words like 'istabraq' and 'sijjil' that are not originally Arabic. Muslim scholars have robust responses that affirm the Quran's Arabicness while accounting for such vocabulary. In this lesson, we examine the objection and its three classical refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection is phrased as a rhetorical question: 'How can it be valid that it is a clear Arabic?'
The objector uses four specific examples: al-istabraq, sijjil, al-qistas, al-maqalid.
2
Identify the three refutations
The passage gives three distinct answers: (1) agreement between languages, (2) Arabic composition, (3) predominance (taghlib). Work through each one and understand the reasoning.
These refutations are not mutually exclusive; they offer different perspectives.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why is it important that the Quran is considered Arabic? How do these refutations preserve the Quran's linguistic miracle?
This connects to the broader doctrine of I'jaz al-Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منها أن فيه كلمات غير عربية كالإستبرق ، والسجيل ، والقسطاس ، والمقاليد . فكيف يصح أنه عربي مبين؟ فرد بأن ذلك من توافق اللغتين، أو المراد أنه عربي النظم والتركيب أو الكل عربي على سبيل التغليب
Among them is that it contains non-Arabic words like al-istabraq (silk brocade), sijjil (baked clay), al-qistas (balance), and al-maqalid (keys). So how can it be valid that it is a clear Arabic [Quran]? It is refuted by [saying] that this is due to agreement between the two languages, or what is meant is that it is Arabic in its composition and structure, or all of it is Arabic by predominance [ghalaba].
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection: the Quran contains words that are not originally Arabic, such as 'istabraq', 'sijjil', 'qistas', and 'maqalid'. The objector argues that if the Quran claims to be an Arabic revelation, how can it include foreign words? The refutation (al-radd) gives three possible answers: (1) these words exist in both languages due to linguistic overlap, so they are not exclusively foreign; (2) the Quran is Arabic in its overall composition and structure, even if some individual words are originally from other tongues; (3) the Quran is considered Arabic by way of predominance (taghlib), meaning the majority is Arabic and the rest follows it in ruling.

Key Terms
كلمات غير عربية
توافق اللغتين
تغليب
إستبرق
سجيل
قسطاس
مقاليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any non-Arabic word in the Quran would make the Quran non-Arabic.
Correction: The Quran is Arabic in its composition, structure, and predominance; a few originally non-Arabic words do not change its Arabicness.

Checkpoint
What is one of the refutations given for the presence of non-Arabic words in the Quran?
A) The words are not actually present.
B) The Quran is Arabic in its composition and structure, not necessarily every word.
C) Arabic originated from those words.
D) The Quran was revealed in multiple languages.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's Arabicness is affirmed by its composition, structure, and the principle of predominance, not by every word being originally Arabic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever harbored a quiet doubt about the Quran's authenticity because of words like 'istabraq' or 'sijjil'? Now that you know the refutations, will you actively trust the scholars' explanations and let this strengthen your certainty?
?
The principle of taghlib (predominance) teaches that a few exceptions don't define the whole. Where in your own life are you allowing a handful of mistakes or failures to overshadow the overwhelming good that Allah has placed in you? Will you consciously apply this lens of predominance to your self-worth and see yourself as a believer striving, not defined by slips?
?
The refutation that the Quran is Arabic in composition, not every word, invites you to look at the bigger picture. Are you approaching the Quran's message as a coherent whole, or getting stuck on isolated details? This week, will you read one surah with the intention of grasping its overarching theme rather than picking apart individual words?
